<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">History</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Study of the past</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the academic discipline. For a general history of human beings, see <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">Human history</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/History_(disambiguation)" title="History (disambiguation)">History (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2978" data-file-width="2232" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marble_bust_of_Herodotos_MET_DT11742_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 484 BC</span>–<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 425 BC</span>), often considered the "father of history" in the Western world</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:250px; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Those who cannot remember the past are condemned to repeat it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/George_Santayana" title="George Santayana">George Santayana</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p><b>History</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%B1%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%81%CE%AF%CE%B1#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ἱστορία">ἱστορία</a></span></span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">historíā</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'inquiry; knowledge acquired by investigation')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JosephJanda_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-JosephJanda-2">[2]</a></sup> is the study and the documentation of the <a href="/wiki/Past" title="Past">past</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Events before the <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing#Inventions_of_writing" title="History of writing">invention of writing systems</a> are considered <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>. "History" is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umbrella_term" title="Umbrella term">umbrella term</a> comprising past events as well as the memory, discovery, collection, organization, presentation, and interpretation of these events. <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">Historians</a> seek knowledge of the past using <a href="/wiki/Historical_source" title="Historical source">historical sources</a> such as written documents, oral accounts, art and material artifacts, and ecological markers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>History is also an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discipline_(academia)" title="Discipline (academia)">academic discipline</a> which uses <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a> to describe, examine, question, and analyze past events, and investigate their patterns of cause and effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-evans1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Historians often debate which narrative best explains an event, as well as the significance of different causes and effects. Historians also debate the <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">nature of history</a> as an end in itself, as well as its usefulness to give perspective on the problems of the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-evans1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tosh1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tosh1-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Stories common to a particular culture, but not supported by external sources (such as the tales surrounding <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>), are usually classified as <a href="/wiki/Cultural_heritage" title="Cultural heritage">cultural heritage</a> or <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">legends</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Low1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Low1-12">[12]</a></sup> History differs from <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myth</a> in that it is supported by verifiable <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a>. However, ancient cultural influences have helped spawn variant interpretations of the nature of history which have evolved over the centuries and continue to change today. The modern study of history is wide-ranging, and includes the study of specific regions and the study of certain topical or thematic elements of historical investigation. History is often taught as a part of primary and secondary education, and the academic study of history is a <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_academic_disciplines#History" title="Outline of academic disciplines">major discipline</a> in university studies.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, a 5th-century BC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_historiography" title="Greek historiography">Greek historian</a>, is often considered the "father of history" in the Western tradition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fordham_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-fordham-13">[13]</a></sup> although he has also been criticized as the "father of lies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Along with his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>, he helped form the foundations for the modern study of past events and societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Their works continue to be read today, and the gap between the culture-focused Herodotus and the military-focused Thucydides remains a point of contention or approach in modern historical writing. In East Asia, a state <a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">chronicle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_Annals" title="Spring and Autumn Annals">Spring and Autumn Annals</a>, was reputed to date from as early as 722 BC, although only 2nd-century BC texts have survived.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2378" data-file-width="5126" decoding="async" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg/250px-History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg/375px-History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg/500px-History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:History-Dielman-Highsmith.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>History</i> by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Dielman" title="Frederick Dielman">Frederick Dielman</a> (1896)</div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>history</i> comes from <i>historía</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">ἱστορία</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">historíā</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'inquiry, knowledge from inquiry, or judge'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>). It was in that sense that <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> used the word in his <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Animals" title="History of Animals">History of Animals</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferrater-Mora_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrater-Mora-18">[18]</a></sup> The ancestor word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἵστωρ</span></span> is attested early on in <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymns" title="Homeric Hymns">Homeric Hymns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athenian" title="Athenian">Athenian</a> <a href="/wiki/Ephebos" title="Ephebos">ephebes</a>' oath, and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boeotic" title="Boeotic">Boeotic</a> inscriptions (in a legal sense, either "judge" or "witness", or similar). The Greek word was borrowed into Classical Latin as <i>historia</i>, meaning "investigation, inquiry, research, account, description, written account of past events, writing of history, historical narrative, recorded knowledge of past events, story, narrative". <i>History</i> was borrowed from Latin (possibly via <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a>) into <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> as <i>stær</i> ("history, narrative, story"), but this word fell out of use in the late Old English period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_Online._Oxford_University_Press_2014_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED_Online._Oxford_University_Press_2014-19">[19]</a></sup> Meanwhile, as Latin became <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> (and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman</a>), <i>historia</i> developed into forms such as <i>istorie</i>, <i>estoire</i>, and <i>historie</i>, with new developments in the meaning: "account of the events of a person's life (beginning of the 12th century), chronicle, account of events as relevant to a group of people or people in general (1155), dramatic or pictorial representation of historical events (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1240</span>), body of knowledge relative to human evolution, science (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1265</span>), narrative of real or imaginary events, story (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1462</span>)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_Online._Oxford_University_Press_2014_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED_Online._Oxford_University_Press_2014-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was from Anglo-Norman that <i>history</i> was borrowed into <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>, and this time the loan stuck. It appears in the 13th-century <i><a href="/wiki/Ancrene_Wisse" title="Ancrene Wisse">Ancrene Wisse</a></i>, but seems to have become a common word in the late 14th century, with an early attestation appearing in <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">John Gower</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Confessio_Amantis" title="Confessio Amantis">Confessio Amantis</a></i> of the 1390s (VI.1383): "I finde in a bok compiled | To this matiere an old histoire, | The which comth nou to mi memoire". In <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>, the meaning of <i>history</i> was "story" in general. The restriction to the meaning "the branch of knowledge that deals with past events; the formal record or study of past events, esp. human affairs" arose in the mid-15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_Online._Oxford_University_Press_2014_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-OED_Online._Oxford_University_Press_2014-19">[19]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, older senses of the word were revived, and it was in the Greek sense that <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> used the term in the late 16th century, when he wrote about <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a>. For him, <i>historia</i> was "the knowledge of objects determined by space and time", that sort of knowledge provided by <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> (while <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> was provided by <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a> was provided by <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In an expression of the linguistic <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">synthetic vs. analytic/isolating dichotomy</a>, English like Chinese (史 vs. 诌) now designates separate words for human history and <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">storytelling</a> in general. In modern <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, and most Germanic and <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, which are solidly synthetic and highly inflected, the same word is still used to mean both "history" and "story". <i>Historian</i> in the sense of a "researcher of history" is attested from 1531. In all <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European languages</a>, the substantive <i>history</i> is still used to mean both "what happened with men", and "the scholarly study of the happened", the latter sense sometimes distinguished with a capital letter, or the word <i><a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferrater-Mora_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrater-Mora-18">[18]</a></sup> The adjective <i>historical</i> is attested from 1661, and <i>historic</i> from 1669.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitney_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitney-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1254" data-file-width="855" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg/220px-The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg/330px-The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg/440px-The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World_-_Title_Page.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The title page to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Historians%27_History_of_the_World" title="The Historians' History of the World">The Historians' History of the World</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Historians write in the context of their own time, and with due regard to the current dominant ideas of how to interpret the past, and sometimes write to provide lessons for their own society. In the words of <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Croce" title="Benedetto Croce">Benedetto Croce</a>, "All history is contemporary history". History is facilitated by the formation of a "true discourse of past" through the production of narrative and analysis of past events relating to the human race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The modern discipline of history is dedicated to the institutional production of this discourse.
</p><p>All events that are remembered and preserved in some authentic form constitute the historical record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wordnet_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-wordnet-23">[23]</a></sup> The task of historical discourse is to identify the sources which can most usefully contribute to the production of accurate accounts of past. Therefore, the constitution of the historian's archive is a result of circumscribing a more general archive by invalidating the usage of certain texts and documents (by falsifying their claims to represent the "true past"). Part of the historian's role is to skillfully and objectively utilize the vast amount of sources from the past, most often found in the archives. The process of creating a narrative inevitably generates a silence as historians remember or emphasize different events of the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="What is the silence spoken of? (August 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The study of history has sometimes been classified as part of the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a> and at other times as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> It can also be seen as a bridge between those two broad areas, incorporating methodologies from both. Some individual historians strongly support one or the other classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In the 20th century the <a href="/wiki/Annales_school" title="Annales school">Annales school</a> revolutionized the study of history, by using such outside disciplines as <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a> in the study of global history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, historians have recorded events of the past, either in writing or by passing on an <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a>, and have attempted to answer historical questions through the study of written documents and oral accounts. From the beginning, historians have also used such sources as monuments, inscriptions, and pictures. In general, the sources of historical knowledge can be separated into three categories: what is written, what is said, and what is physically preserved, and historians often consult all three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> But writing is the marker that separates history from what comes before.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archeology" title="Archeology">Archeology</a> is especially helpful in unearthing buried sites and objects, which contribute to the study of history. Archeological finds rarely stand alone, with narrative sources complementing its discoveries. Archeology's methodologies and approaches are independent from the field of history. "Historical archaeology" is a specific branch of archeology which often contrasts its conclusions against those of contemporary textual sources. For example, Mark Leone, the excavator and interpreter of historical <a href="/wiki/Annapolis,_Maryland" title="Annapolis, Maryland">Annapolis, Maryland</a>, US, has sought to understand the contradiction between textual documents idealizing "liberty" and the material record, demonstrating the possession of slaves and the inequalities of wealth made apparent by the study of the total historical environment.
</p><p>There are varieties of ways in which history can be organized, including chronologically, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culturally</a>, territorially, and thematically. These divisions are not mutually exclusive, and significant intersections are often present. It is possible for historians to concern themselves with both the very specific and the very general, although the modern trend has been toward specialization. The area called <a href="/wiki/Big_History" title="Big History">Big History</a> resists this specialization, and searches for universal patterns or trends. History has often been studied with some practical or <a href="/wiki/Theory" title="Theory">theoretical</a> aim, but also may be studied out of simple intellectual curiosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham-ch1_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-graham-ch1-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
