<span style="font-size: 120%;">↓</span> <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">Future</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047488" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="nobold"><span style="font-size:85%;"><span class="nowrap">  </span>(<a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene epoch</a>)</span></span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:#f8eaba;padding:0.4em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_history_and_prehistory" title="Template:Human history and prehistory"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_history_and_prehistory" title="Template talk:Human history and prehistory"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_history_and_prehistory&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protohistory" title="Protohistory">Protohistory</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">history of the world</a> is the memory of the past <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens sapiens">Homo sapiens sapiens</a></i> around the world, as that experience has been preserved, largely in written records. By "prehistory", historians mean the recovery of knowledge of the past in an area where no written records exist, or where the writing of a culture is not understood. By studying painting, drawings, carvings, and other artifacts, some information can be recovered even in the absence of a written record. Since the 20th century, the study of prehistory is considered essential to avoid history's implicit exclusion of certain civilizations, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> America. Historians in the West have been criticized for focusing disproportionately on the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1961, British historian <a href="/wiki/E._H._Carr" title="E. H. Carr">E. H. Carr</a> wrote:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The line of demarcation between prehistoric and historical times is crossed when people cease to live only in the present, and become consciously interested both in their past and in their future. History begins with the handing down of tradition; and tradition means the carrying of the habits and lessons of the past into the future. Records of the past begin to be kept for the benefit of future generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>This definition includes within the scope of history the strong interests of peoples, such as <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a> and New Zealand <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a> in the past, and the oral records maintained and transmitted to succeeding generations, even before their contact with European civilization.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">Historiography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guicciardini_M_Francesco_La_Historia_dItalia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Guicciardini_M_Francesco_La_Historia_dItalia.jpg/220px-Guicciardini_M_Francesco_La_Historia_dItalia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/Guicciardini_M_Francesco_La_Historia_dItalia.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guicciardini_M_Francesco_La_Historia_dItalia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The title page to <i>La Historia d'Italia</i></div></div></div>
<p>Historiography has a number of related meanings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-culturahistorica.org_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-culturahistorica.org-32">[32]</a></sup> Firstly, it can refer to how history has been produced: the story of the development of <a href="/wiki/Historical_method" title="Historical method">methodology</a> and practices (for example, the move from short-term biographical narrative toward long-term thematic analysis). Secondly, it can refer to what has been produced: a specific body of historical writing (for example, "medieval historiography during the 1960s" means "Works of medieval history written during the 1960s").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-culturahistorica.org_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-culturahistorica.org-32">[32]</a></sup> Thirdly, it may refer to why history is produced: the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_history" title="Philosophy of history">philosophy of history</a>. As a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meta-level" title="Meta-level">meta-level</a> analysis of descriptions of the past, this third conception can relate to the first two in that the analysis usually focuses on the narratives, interpretations, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_view" title="World view">world view</a>, use of evidence, or method of presentation of other historians. Professional historians also debate the question of whether history can be taught as a single coherent narrative or a series of competing narratives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_methods">Historical methods</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Historical_method" title="Historical method">Historical method</a></div>
<table class="toccolours" style="float:right; margin:0 0 1em 1em; width:250px; text-align:left; clear:right;">
<tbody><tr>
<td><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancientlibraryalex.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1090" data-file-width="1072" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ancientlibraryalex.jpg/220px-Ancientlibraryalex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ancientlibraryalex.jpg/330px-Ancientlibraryalex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ancientlibraryalex.jpg/440px-Ancientlibraryalex.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancientlibraryalex.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A depiction of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a></div></div></div>
<div style="background:#f8eaba; text-align:center;"> Historical method basics </div>
<p>The following questions are used by historians in modern work.
</p>
<ol><li><i>When was</i> the source, written or unwritten, produced (<a href="/wiki/Calendar_date" title="Calendar date">date</a>)?</li>
<li>Where was it produced (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/localize" title="wikt:localize">localization</a>)?</li>
<li>By whom was it produced (<a href="/wiki/Author" title="Author">authorship</a>)?</li>
<li>From what pre-existing material was it produced (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/analysis" title="wikt:analysis">analysis</a>)?</li>
<li>In what original form was it produced (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/integrity" title="wikt:integrity">integrity</a>)?</li>
<li>What is the evidential value of its contents (<a href="/wiki/Credibility" title="Credibility">credibility</a>)?</li></ol>
<p>The first four are known as <a href="/wiki/Historical_criticism" title="Historical criticism">historical criticism</a>; the fifth, <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">textual criticism</a>; and, together, external criticism. The sixth and final inquiry about a source is called internal criticism.
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The historical method comprises the techniques and guidelines by which <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historians</a> use <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">primary sources</a> and other evidence to research and then to <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">write history</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">Halicarnassus</a> (484 BC–<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 425 BC</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lamberg-karlovsky-p5_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-lamberg-karlovsky-p5-35">[35]</a></sup> has generally been acclaimed as the "father of history". However, his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a> (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 460 BC</span>–<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 400 BC</span>) is credited with having first approached history with a well-developed historical method in his work the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Peloponnesian_War" title="History of the Peloponnesian War">History of the Peloponnesian War</a></i>. Thucydides, unlike Herodotus, regarded history as being the product of the choices and actions of human beings, and looked at <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">cause and effect</a>, rather than as the result of divine intervention (though Herodotus was not wholly committed to this idea himself).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lamberg-karlovsky-p5_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-lamberg-karlovsky-p5-35">[35]</a></sup> In his historical method, Thucydides emphasized chronology, a nominally neutral point of view, and that the human world was the result of the actions of human beings. Greek historians also viewed history as <a href="/wiki/Historic_recurrence" title="Historic recurrence">cyclical</a>, with events regularly recurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lamberg-karlovsky-p6_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-lamberg-karlovsky-p6-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were historical traditions and sophisticated use of historical method in ancient and medieval <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. The groundwork for professional historiography in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> court historian known as <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> (145–90 BC), author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> (<i>Shiji</i>). For the quality of his written work, Sima Qian is posthumously known as the Father of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">Chinese historiography</a>. Chinese historians of subsequent dynastic periods in China used his <i>Shiji</i> as the official format for <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">historical texts</a>, as well as for biographical literature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Saint Augustine</a> was influential in <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Christian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_thought" title="Western thought">Western thought</a> at the beginning of the medieval period. Through the Medieval and <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> periods, history was often studied through a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_history" title="Sacred history">sacred</a> or religious perspective. Around 1800, German philosopher and historian <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and a more <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">secular</a> approach in historical study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham-ch1_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-graham-ch1-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the preface to his book, the <i><a href="/wiki/Muqaddimah" title="Muqaddimah">Muqaddimah</a></i> (1377), the <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_early_Islam" title="Historiography of early Islam">Arab historian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sociology_in_medieval_Islam" title="Sociology in medieval Islam">early sociologist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a>, warned of seven mistakes that he thought that historians regularly committed. In this criticism, he approached the past as strange and in need of interpretation. The originality of Ibn Khaldun was to claim that the cultural difference of another age must govern the evaluation of relevant historical material, to distinguish the principles according to which it might be possible to attempt the evaluation, and lastly, to feel the need for experience, in addition to rational principles, in order to assess a culture of the past. Ibn Khaldun often criticized "idle <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a> and uncritical acceptance of historical data". As a result, he introduced a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to the study of history, and he often referred to it as his "new science".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> His historical method also laid the groundwork for the observation of the role of <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Systemic_bias" title="Systemic bias">systematic bias</a> in history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mowlana_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mowlana-38">[38]</a></sup> and he is thus considered to be the "father of historiography"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enan_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enan-40">[40]</a></sup> or the "father of the philosophy of history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Akhtar_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Akhtar-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the West, historians developed modern methods of historiography in the 17th and 18th centuries, especially in France and Germany. In 1851, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a> summarized these methods:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>
From the successive strata of our historical deposits, they [Historians] diligently gather all the highly colored fragments, pounce upon everything that is curious and sparkling and chuckle like children over their glittering acquisitions; meanwhile the rich veins of wisdom that ramify amidst this worthless debris, lie utterly neglected. Cumbrous volumes of rubbish are greedily accumulated, while those masses of rich ore, that should have been dug out, and from which golden truths might have been smelted, are left untaught and unsought<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>By the "rich ore" Spencer meant scientific theory of history. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Thomas_Buckle" title="Henry Thomas Buckle">Henry Thomas Buckle</a> expressed a dream of history becoming one day science:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>
In regard to nature, events apparently the most irregular and capricious have been explained and have been shown to be in accordance with certain fixed and universal laws. This have been done because men of ability and, above all, men of patient, untiring thought have studied events with the view of discovering their regularity, and if human events were subject to a similar treatment, we have every right to expect similar results<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Contrary to Buckle's dream, the 19th-century historian with greatest influence on methods became <a href="/wiki/Leopold_von_Ranke" title="Leopold von Ranke">Leopold von Ranke</a> in Germany. He limited history to "what really happened" and by this directed the field further away from science. For Ranke, historical data should be collected carefully, examined objectively and put together with critical rigor. But these procedures "are merely the prerequisites and preliminaries of science. The heart of science is searching out order and regularity in the data being examined and in formulating generalizations or laws about them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>
As Historians like Ranke and many who followed him have pursued it, <i>no</i>, history is not a science. Thus if Historians tell us that, given the manner in which he practices his craft, it cannot be considered a science, we must take him at his word. If he is not doing science, then, whatever else he is doing, he is not <i>doing</i> science. The traditional Historian is thus no scientist and history, as conventionally practiced, is not a science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In the 20th century, academic historians focused less on epic nationalistic narratives, which often tended to glorify the nation or <a href="/wiki/Great_man_theory" title="Great man theory">great men</a>, to more objective and complex analyses of social and intellectual forces. A major trend of historical methodology in the 20th century was a tendency to treat history more as a <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social science</a> rather than as an <a href="/wiki/The_arts" title="The arts">art</a>, which traditionally had been the case. Some of the leading advocates of history as a social science were a diverse collection of scholars which included <a href="/wiki/Fernand_Braudel" title="Fernand Braudel">Fernand Braudel</a>, <a href="/wiki/E._H._Carr" title="E. H. Carr">E. H. Carr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Fischer" title="Fritz Fischer">Fritz Fischer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Le_Roy_Ladurie" title="Emmanuel Le Roy Ladurie">Emmanuel Le Roy Ladurie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans-Ulrich_Wehler" title="Hans-Ulrich Wehler">Hans-Ulrich Wehler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Trigger" title="Bruce Trigger">Bruce Trigger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc_Bloch" title="Marc Bloch">Marc Bloch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Dietrich_Bracher" title="Karl Dietrich Bracher">Karl Dietrich Bracher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gay" title="Peter Gay">Peter Gay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fogel" title="Robert Fogel">Robert Fogel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucien_Febvre" title="Lucien Febvre">Lucien Febvre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Stone" title="Lawrence Stone">Lawrence Stone</a>. Many of the advocates of history as a social science were or are noted for their multidisciplinary approach. Braudel combined history with geography, Bracher history with political science, Fogel history with economics, Gay history with psychology, Trigger history with archeology, while Wehler, Bloch, Fischer, Stone, Febvre, and Le Roy Ladurie have in varying and differing ways amalgamated history with sociology, geography, anthropology, and economics. Nevertheless, these multidisciplinary approaches failed to produce a theory of history. So far only one theory of history came from the pen of a professional Historian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Whatever other theories of history we have, they were written by experts from other fields (for example, Marxian theory of history). More recently, the field of <a href="/wiki/Digital_history" title="Digital history">digital history</a> has begun to address ways of using computer technology to pose new questions to historical data and generate digital scholarship.
</p><p>In sincere opposition to the claims of history as a social science, historians such as <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trevor-Roper" title="Hugh Trevor-Roper">Hugh Trevor-Roper</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Lukacs" title="John Lukacs">John Lukacs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Creighton" title="Donald Creighton">Donald Creighton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_Himmelfarb" title="Gertrude Himmelfarb">Gertrude Himmelfarb</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Ritter" title="Gerhard Ritter">Gerhard Ritter</a> argued that the key to the historians' work was the power of the <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imagination</a>, and hence contended that history should be understood as an art. French historians associated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annales_School" title="Annales School">Annales School</a> introduced quantitative history, using raw data to track the lives of typical individuals, and were prominent in the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_history" title="Cultural history">cultural history</a> (cf. <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_mentalities" title="History of mentalities">histoire des mentalités</a></i>). Intellectual historians such as <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Butterfield" title="Herbert Butterfield">Herbert Butterfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Nolte" title="Ernst Nolte">Ernst Nolte</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Mosse" title="George Mosse">George Mosse</a> have argued for the significance of ideas in history. American historians, motivated by the civil rights era, focused on formerly overlooked ethnic, racial, and socioeconomic groups. Another genre of <a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">social history</a> to emerge in the post-WWII era was <i><a href="/wiki/Alltagsgeschichte" title="Alltagsgeschichte">Alltagsgeschichte</a></i> (History of Everyday Life). Scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Broszat" title="Martin Broszat">Martin Broszat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Kershaw" title="Ian Kershaw">Ian Kershaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Detlev_Peukert" title="Detlev Peukert">Detlev Peukert</a> sought to examine what everyday life was like for ordinary people in 20th-century Germany, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi</a> period.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Marxist_historiography" title="Marxist historiography">Marxist historians</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Eric_Hobsbawm" title="Eric Hobsbawm">Eric Hobsbawm</a>, <a href="/wiki/E._P._Thompson" title="E. P. Thompson">E. P. Thompson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Hilton" title="Rodney Hilton">Rodney Hilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lefebvre" title="Georges Lefebvre">Georges Lefebvre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Genovese" title="Eugene Genovese">Eugene Genovese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Deutscher" title="Isaac Deutscher">Isaac Deutscher</a>, <a href="/wiki/C._L._R._James" title="C. L. R. James">C. L. R. James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Mason" title="Timothy Mason">Timothy Mason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Aptheker" title="Herbert Aptheker">Herbert Aptheker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arno_J._Mayer" title="Arno J. Mayer">Arno J. Mayer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hill_(historian)" title="Christopher Hill (historian)">Christopher Hill</a> have sought to validate <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>'s theories by analyzing history from a Marxist perspective. In response to the Marxist interpretation of history, historians such as <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Furet" title="François Furet">François Furet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Pipes" title="Richard Pipes">Richard Pipes</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._C._D._Clark" title="J. C. D. Clark">J. C. D. Clark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roland_Mousnier" title="Roland Mousnier">Roland Mousnier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ashby_Turner" title="Henry Ashby Turner">Henry Ashby Turner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Conquest" title="Robert Conquest">Robert Conquest</a> have offered anti-Marxist interpretations of history. <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">Feminist</a> historians such as <a href="/wiki/Joan_Wallach_Scott" title="Joan Wallach Scott">Joan Wallach Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Koonz" title="Claudia Koonz">Claudia Koonz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natalie_Zemon_Davis" title="Natalie Zemon Davis">Natalie Zemon Davis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheila_Rowbotham" title="Sheila Rowbotham">Sheila Rowbotham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gisela_Bock" title="Gisela Bock">Gisela Bock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerda_Lerner" title="Gerda Lerner">Gerda Lerner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Fox-Genovese" title="Elizabeth Fox-Genovese">Elizabeth Fox-Genovese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Hunt" title="Lynn Hunt">Lynn Hunt</a> have argued for the importance of studying the experience of women in the past. In recent years, <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernists</a> have challenged the validity and need for the study of history on the basis that all history is based on the personal interpretation of sources. In his 1997 book <i>In Defence of History</i>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_J._Evans" title="Richard J. Evans">Richard J. Evans</a> defended the worth of history. Another defense of history from postmodernist criticism was the Australian historian <a href="/wiki/Keith_Windschuttle" title="Keith Windschuttle">Keith Windschuttle</a>'s 1994 book, <i>The Killing of History</i>.
</p><p>Today, most historians begin their research process in the archives, on either a physical or digital platform. They often propose an argument and use their research to support it. <a href="/wiki/John_H._Arnold_(historian)" title="John H. Arnold (historian)">John H. Arnold</a> proposed that history is an argument, which creates the possibility of creating change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">[5]</a></sup> Digital information companies, such as <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, have sparked controversy over the role of internet censorship in information access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marxian_theory">Marxian theory</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_history" title="Marx's theory of history">Marx's theory of history</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Marxist_philosophy" title="Marxist philosophy">Marxist theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a> theorises that society is fundamentally determined by the <i>material conditions</i> at any given time – in other words, the relationships which people have with each other in order to fulfill basic needs such as feeding, clothing and housing themselves and their families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Overall, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Engels</a> claimed to have identified five successive stages of the development of these material conditions in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marxist_historiography" title="Marxist historiography">Marxist historiography</a> was once orthodoxy in the Soviet Union, but since the collapse of communism there in 1991, Mikhail Krom says it has been reduced to the margins of scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_shortcomings_in_the_production_of_history">Potential shortcomings in the production of history</span></h3>
<p>Many historians believe that the production of history is embedded with <a href="/wiki/Bias" title="Bias">bias</a> because events and known facts in history can be interpreted in a variety of ways. <a href="/wiki/Constantin_Fasolt" title="Constantin Fasolt">Constantin Fasolt</a> suggested that history is linked to politics by the practice of silence itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[51]</a></sup> He also said: "A second common view of the link between history and politics rests on the elementary observation that historians are often influenced by politics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[51]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Michel-Rolph_Trouillot" title="Michel-Rolph Trouillot">Michel-Rolph Trouillot</a>, the historical process is rooted in the archives, therefore silences, or parts of history that are forgotten, may be an intentional part of a narrative strategy that dictates how areas of history are remembered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24">[24]</a></sup> Historical omissions can occur in many ways and can have a profound effect on historical records. Information can also purposely be excluded or left out accidentally. Historians have coined multiple terms that describe the act of omitting historical information, including: "silencing",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24">[24]</a></sup> "selective memory",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[52]</a></sup> and erasures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gerda_Lerner" title="Gerda Lerner">Gerda Lerner</a>, a twentieth century historian who focused much of her work on historical omissions involving women and their accomplishments, explained the negative impact that these omissions had on minority groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Environmental historian <a href="/wiki/William_Cronon" title="William Cronon">William Cronon</a> proposed three ways to combat bias and ensure authentic and accurate narratives: narratives must not contradict known fact, they must make ecological sense (specifically for environmental history), and published work must be reviewed by scholarly community and other historians to ensure accountability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Areas_of_study">Areas of study</span></h2>
<table class="toccolours" style="float:right; margin:0 0 1em 1em; width:250px; text-align:left; clear:right;">
<tbody><tr>
<th style="background:#f8eaba;">Particular studies and fields
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>
<p>These are approaches to history; not listed are histories of other fields, such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">history of science</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematics" title="History of mathematics">history of mathematics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_philosophy" title="History of philosophy">history of philosophy</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Ancient history</a>: the study of history from the beginning of human history until the Early Middle Ages.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_history" title="Atlantic history">Atlantic history</a>: the study of the history of people living on or near the Atlantic Ocean.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_art" title="History of art">Art history</a>: the study of changes in and the social context of art.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Comparative_history" title="Comparative history">Comparative history</a>: the historical analysis of social and cultural entities not confined to national boundaries.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Contemporary_history" title="Contemporary history">Contemporary history</a>: the study of recent historical events.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_history" title="Counterfactual history">Counterfactual history</a>: the study of historical events as they might have happened in different causal circumstances.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cultural_history" title="Cultural history">Cultural history</a>: the study of culture in the past.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Digital_history" title="Digital history">Digital history</a>: the use of computing technologies to do massive searches in published sources.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Economic_history" title="Economic history">Economic history</a>: the use of economic models fitted to the past.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Intellectual_history" title="Intellectual history">Intellectual history</a>: the study of ideas in the context of the cultures that produced them and their development over time.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maritime_history" title="Maritime history">Maritime history</a>: the study of maritime transport and all connected subjects.</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Material_history&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Material history (page does not exist)">Material history</a>: the study of objects and the stories they can tell.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">Modern history</a>: the study of Modern Times, the era after the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Military_history" title="Military history">Military history</a>: the study of warfare, historical wars, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_history" title="Naval history">Naval history</a>, which is sometimes considered to be a sub-branch of military history.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oral_history" title="Oral history">Oral history</a>: the collection and study of historical information by utilizing spoken interviews with people who have lived past events.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Palaeography" title="Palaeography">Palaeography</a>: the study of ancient texts.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/People%27s_history" title="People's history">People's history</a>: historical work from the perspective of common people.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Political_history" title="Political history">Political history</a>: the study of politics in the past.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Psychohistory" title="Psychohistory">Psychohistory</a>: the study of the psychological motivations for historical events.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">Pseudohistory</a>: studies about the past that fall outside the domain of mainstream history (sometimes equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">Social history</a>: the study of the process of social change throughout history.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Women%27s_history" title="Women's history">Women's history</a>: the history of female human beings. <a href="/wiki/Gender_history" title="Gender history">Gender history</a> is related and covers the perspective of gender.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_history" title="World history">World history</a>: the study of history from a global perspective, with special attention to non-Western societies.</li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periods">Periods</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">Periodization</a></div>
<p>Historical study often focuses on events and developments that occur in particular blocks of time. Historians give these <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periods of time</a> names in order to allow "organising ideas and classificatory generalisations" to be used by historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marwick-p169_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marwick-p169-54">[54]</a></sup> The names given to a period can vary with geographical location, as can the dates of the beginning and end of a particular period. <a href="/wiki/Century" title="Century">Centuries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Decade" title="Decade">decades</a> are commonly used periods and the time they represent depends on the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_era" title="Calendar era">dating system</a> used. Most periods are constructed retrospectively and so reflect value judgments made about the past. The way periods are constructed and the names given to them can affect the way they are viewed and studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tosh-p168-169_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tosh-p168-169-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_periodization">Prehistoric periodization</span></h4>
<p>The field of history generally leaves prehistory to archeologists, who have entirely different sets of tools and theories. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archeology" title="Archeology">archeology</a>, the usual method for periodization of the distant <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistoric</a> past is to rely on changes in material culture and technology, such as the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, with subdivisions that are also based on different styles of material remains. Here prehistory is divided into a series of "chapters" so that periods in history could unfold not only in a relative chronology but also narrative chronology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> This narrative content could be in the form of functional-economic interpretation. There are periodizations, however, that do not have this narrative aspect, relying largely on relative chronology, and that are thus devoid of any specific meaning.
</p><p>Despite the development over recent decades of the ability through <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a> and other scientific methods to give actual dates for many sites or artefacts, these long-established schemes seem likely to remain in use. In many cases neighboring cultures with writing have left some history of cultures without it, which may be used. Periodization, however, is not viewed as a perfect framework, with one account explaining that "cultural changes do not conveniently start and stop (combinedly) at periodization boundaries" and that different trajectories of change need to be studied in their own right before they get intertwined with cultural phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_locations">Geographical locations</span></h3>
<p>Particular <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geographical</a> locations can form the basis of historical study, for example, <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Country" title="Country">countries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">cities</a>. Understanding why historic events took place is important. To do this, historians often turn to <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Jules_Michelet" title="Jules Michelet">Jules Michelet</a> in his book <i>Histoire de France</i> (1833), "without geographical basis, the people, the makers of history, seem to be walking on air".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Weather patterns, the water supply, and the landscape of a place all affect the lives of the people who live there. For example, to explain why the ancient Egyptians developed a successful civilization, studying the <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Egypt" title="Geography of Egypt">geography of Egypt</a> is essential. Egyptian civilization was built on the banks of the Nile River, which flooded each year, depositing soil on its banks. The rich soil could help farmers grow enough crops to feed the people in the cities. That meant everyone did not have to farm, so some people could perform other jobs that helped develop the civilization. There is also the case of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_determinism" title="Environmental determinism">climate</a>, which historians like <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Huntington" title="Ellsworth Huntington">Ellsworth Huntington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Churchill_Semple" title="Ellen Churchill Semple">Ellen Churchill Semple</a> cited as a crucial influence on the course of history. Huntington and Semple further argued that climate has an impact on racial temperament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions">Regions</span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Africa" title="History of Africa">History of Africa</a> begins with the first emergence of modern human beings on the continent, continuing into its modern present as a patchwork of diverse and politically developing nation states.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Americas" title="History of the Americas">History of the Americas</a> is the collective history of North and South America, including Central America and the Caribbean.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_North_America" title="History of North America">History of North America</a> is the study of the past passed down from generation to generation on the continent in the Earth's northern and western hemisphere.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Central_America" title="History of Central America">History of Central America</a> is the study of the past passed down from generation to generation on the continent in the Earth's western hemisphere.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Caribbean" title="History of the Caribbean">History of the Caribbean</a> begins with the oldest evidence where 7,000-year-old remains have been found.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_South_America" title="History of South America">History of South America</a> is the study of the past passed down from generation to generation on the continent in the Earth's southern and western hemisphere.</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Antarctica" title="History of Antarctica">History of Antarctica</a> emerges from early Western theories of a vast continent, known as Terra Australis, believed to exist in the far south of the globe.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Eurasia" title="History of Eurasia">History of Eurasia</a> is the collective history of several distinct peripheral coastal regions: the Middle East, South Asia, East Asia, Southeast Asia, and Europe, linked by the interior mass of the Eurasian steppe of Central Asia and Eastern Europe.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">History of Europe</a> describes the passage of time from humans inhabiting the European continent to the present day.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Asia" title="History of Asia">History of Asia</a> can be seen as the collective history of several distinct peripheral coastal regions, East Asia, South Asia, and the Middle East linked by the interior mass of the Eurasian steppe.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_East_Asia" title="History of East Asia">History of East Asia</a> is the study of the past passed down from generation to generation in East Asia.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Middle_East" title="History of the Middle East">History of the Middle East</a> begins with the earliest civilizations in the region now known as the Middle East that were established around 3000 BC, in Mesopotamia (Iraq).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">History of India</a> is the study of the past passed down from generation to generation in the Sub-Himalayan region.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Southeast_Asia" title="History of Southeast Asia">History of Southeast Asia</a> has been characterized as interaction between regional players and foreign powers.</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Oceania" title="History of Oceania">History of Oceania</a> is the collective history of Australia, New Zealand and the Pacific Islands.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia" title="History of Australia">History of Australia</a> starts with the documentation of the Makassar trading with Indigenous Australians on Australia's north coast.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_New_Zealand" title="History of New Zealand">History of New Zealand</a> dates back at least 700 years to when it was discovered and settled by Polynesians, who developed a distinct Māori culture centered on kinship links and land.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Pacific_Islands" title="History of the Pacific Islands">History of the Pacific Islands</a> covers the history of the islands in the Pacific Ocean.</li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_history" title="Military history">Military history</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Military_history" title="Military history">Military history</a> concerns warfare, strategies, battles, weapons, and the psychology of combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The "new military history" since the 1970s has been concerned with soldiers more than generals, with psychology more than tactics, and with the broader impact of warfare on society and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pavkovic_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pavkovic-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious">Religious</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">History of religion</a></div>
<p>The history of religion has been a main theme for both secular and religious historians for centuries, and continues to be taught in seminaries and academe. Leading journals include <i><a href="/wiki/Church_History_(journal)" title="Church History (journal)">Church History</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Catholic_Historical_Review" title="The Catholic Historical Review">The Catholic Historical Review</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Religions_(journal)" title="History of Religions (journal)">History of Religions</a></i>. Topics range widely from political and cultural and artistic dimensions, to theology and liturgy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> This subject studies religions from all regions and areas of the world where humans have lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social">Social</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">Social history</a></div>
<p><i>Social history</i>, sometimes called the <i>new social history</i>, is the field that includes history of ordinary people and their strategies and institutions for coping with life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In its "golden age" it was a major growth field in the 1960s and 1970s among scholars, and still is well represented in history departments. In two decades from 1975 to 1995, the proportion of professors of history in American universities identifying with social history rose from 31% to 41%, while the proportion of political historians fell from 40% to 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In the history departments of British universities in 2007, of the 5723 faculty members, 1644 (29%) identified themselves with social history while <a href="/wiki/Political_history" title="Political history">political history</a> came next with 1425 (25%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The "old" social history before the 1960s was a hodgepodge of topics without a central theme, and it often included political movements, like Populism, that were "social" in the sense of being outside the elite system. Social history was contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Political_history" title="Political history">political history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_history" title="Intellectual history">intellectual history</a> and the history of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Man_theory" title="Great Man theory">great men</a>. English historian <a href="/wiki/G._M._Trevelyan" title="G. M. Trevelyan">G. M. Trevelyan</a> saw it as the bridging point between economic and political history, reflecting that, "Without social history, economic history is barren and political history unintelligible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> While the field has often been viewed negatively as history with the politics left out, it has also been defended as "history with the people put back in".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Subfields">Subfields</span></h4>
<p>The chief subfields of social history include:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Black_history_(disambiguation)" title="Black history (disambiguation)">Black history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Demographic_history" title="Demographic history">Demographic history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ethnic_history" title="Ethnic history">Ethnic history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gender_history" title="Gender history">Gender history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_childhood" title="History of childhood">History of childhood</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_education" title="History of education">History of education</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_family" title="History of the family">History of the family</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labor_history_(discipline)" title="Labor history (discipline)">Labor history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/LGBT_history" title="LGBT history">LGBT history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rural_history" title="Rural history">Rural history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Urban_history" title="Urban history">Urban history</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/American_urban_history" title="American urban history">American urban history</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Women%27s_history" title="Women's history">Women's history</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural">Cultural</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_history" title="Cultural history">Cultural history</a></div>
<p>Cultural history replaced <a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">social history</a> as the dominant form in the 1980s and 1990s. It typically combines the approaches of anthropology and history to look at language, popular cultural traditions and cultural interpretations of historical experience. It examines the records and narrative descriptions of past knowledge, customs, and arts of a group of people. How peoples constructed their memory of the past is a major topic. Cultural history includes the study of <a href="/wiki/History_of_art" title="History of art">art in society</a> as well is the study of images and human visual production (<a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomatic">Diplomatic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_history" title="Diplomatic history">Diplomatic history</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_history" title="Diplomatic history">Diplomatic history</a> focuses on the relationships between nations, primarily regarding diplomacy and the causes of wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> More recently it looks at the causes of peace and human rights. It typically presents the viewpoints of the foreign office, and long-term strategic values, as the driving force of continuity and change in history. This type of <i>political history</i> is the study of the conduct of <a href="/wiki/International_relations#History_of_International_Relations" title="International relations">international relations</a> between states or across state boundaries over time. Historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muriel_Chamberlain" title="Muriel Chamberlain">Muriel Chamberlain</a> notes that after the First World War, "diplomatic history replaced constitutional history as the flagship of historical investigation, at once the most important, most exact and most sophisticated of historical studies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> She adds that after 1945, the trend reversed, allowing social history to replace it.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history" title="Economic history">Economic history</a> and <a href="/wiki/Business_history" title="Business history">Business history</a></div>
<p>Although economic history has been well established since the late 19th century, in recent years academic studies have shifted more and more toward economics departments and away from traditional history departments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Business_history" title="Business history">Business history</a> deals with the history of individual business organizations, business methods, government regulation, labour relations, and impact on society. It also includes biographies of individual companies, executives, and entrepreneurs. It is related to economic history. Business history is most often taught in business schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental">Environmental</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_history" title="Environmental history">Environmental history</a></div>
<p>Environmental history is a new field that emerged in the 1980s to look at the history of the environment, especially in the long run, and the impact of human activities upon it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> It is an offshoot of the environmental movement, which was kickstarted by Rachel Carson's <i>Silent Spring</i> in the 1960s.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World">World</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/World_history" title="World history">World history</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">Human history</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_history" title="Universal history">Universal history</a></div>
<p>World history is the study of major civilizations over the last 3000 years or so. World history is primarily a teaching field, rather than a research field. It gained popularity in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Japan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and other countries after the 1980s with the realization that students need a broader exposure to the world as globalization proceeds.
</p><p>It has led to highly controversial interpretations by <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Spengler" title="Oswald Spengler">Oswald Spengler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arnold_J._Toynbee" title="Arnold J. Toynbee">Arnold J. Toynbee</a>, among others.
</p><p>The World History Association publishes the <i>Journal of World History</i> every quarter since 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The H-World discussion list<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> serves as a network of communication among practitioners of world history, with discussions among scholars, announcements, syllabi, bibliographies and book reviews.
</p>
<h3><span id="People.27s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="People's">People's</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/People%27s_history" title="People's history">People's history</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/People%27s_history" title="People's history">people's history</a> is a type of historical work which attempts to account for historical events from the <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">perspective of common people</a>. A people's history is the history of the world that is the story of mass movements and of the outsiders. Individuals or groups not included in the past in other types of writing about history are the primary focus, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement" title="Disfranchisement">disenfranchised</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">oppressed</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture" title="Counterculture">nonconformists</a>, and the otherwise forgotten people. The authors are typically on the left and have a socialist model in mind, as in the approach of the <a href="/wiki/History_Workshop_Journal" title="History Workshop Journal">History Workshop</a> movement in Britain in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intellectual">Intellectual</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_history" title="Intellectual history">Intellectual history</a></div>
<p>Intellectual history and the history of ideas emerged in the mid-20th century, with the focus on the intellectuals and their books on the one hand, and on the other the study of ideas as disembodied objects with a career of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Gender_history" title="Gender history">Gender history</a> and <a href="/wiki/LGBT_history" title="LGBT history">LGBT history</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gender_history" title="Gender history">Gender history</a> is a subfield of History and <a href="/wiki/Gender_studies" title="Gender studies">Gender studies</a>, which looks at the past from the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>. The outgrowth of gender history from <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_history" title="Women's history">women's history</a> stemmed from many non-<a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminist</a> historians dismissing the importance of women in history. According to Joan W. Scott, "Gender is a constitutive element of social relationships based on perceived differences between the sexes, and gender is a primary way of signifying relations of power",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> meaning that gender historians study the social effects of perceived differences between the sexes and how all genders utilize allotted power in societal and political structures. Despite being a relatively new field, gender history has had a significant effect on the general study of history. Gender history traditionally differs from women's history in its inclusion of all aspects of gender such as masculinity and femininity, and today's gender history extends to include people who identify outside of that binary. 
<a href="/wiki/LGBT_history" title="LGBT history">LGBT history</a> deals with the first recorded instances of same-sex love and sexuality of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient civilizations</a>, and involves the history of <a href="/wiki/Lesbian" title="Lesbian">lesbian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gay" title="Gay">gay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bisexuality" title="Bisexuality">bisexual</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender</a> (<a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a>) peoples and cultures around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public">Public</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Public_history" title="Public history">Public history</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Public_history" title="Public history">Public history</a> describes the broad range of activities undertaken by people with some training in the discipline of history who are generally working outside of specialized academic settings. Public history practice has quite deep roots in the areas of historic preservation, archival science, oral history, museum curatorship, and other related fields. The term itself began to be used in the U.S. and Canada in the late 1970s, and the field has become increasingly professionalized since that time. Some of the most common settings for public history are museums, historic homes and <a href="/wiki/Historic_site" title="Historic site">historic sites</a>, parks, battlefields, archives, film and television companies, and all levels of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historians">Historians</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a more comprehensive list, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_historians" title="List of historians">List of historians</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B.Croce.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2392" data-file-width="1672" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/B.Croce.jpg/170px-B.Croce.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/B.Croce.jpg/255px-B.Croce.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/B.Croce.jpg/340px-B.Croce.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B.Croce.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Croce" title="Benedetto Croce">Benedetto Croce</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ban_Zhao.jpg"><img alt="Ban Zhao, courtesy name Huiban, was the first known female Chinese historian." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1578" data-file-width="636" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Ban_Zhao.jpg/130px-Ban_Zhao.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Ban_Zhao.jpg/195px-Ban_Zhao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Ban_Zhao.jpg/260px-Ban_Zhao.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ban_Zhao.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ban_Zhao" title="Ban Zhao">Ban Zhao</a>, courtesy name Huiban, was the first known female Chinese historian.</div></div></div>
<p>Professional and amateur historians discover, collect, organize, and present information about past events. They discover this information through archeological evidence, written primary sources, verbal stories or oral histories, and other archival material. In <a href="/wiki/List_of_historians" title="List of historians">lists of historians</a>, historians can be grouped by order of the historical period in which they were writing, which is not necessarily the same as the period in which they specialized. <a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">Chroniclers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Annals" title="Annals">annalists</a>, though they are not historians in the true sense, are also frequently included.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Judgement">Judgement</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ash_heap_of_history" title="Ash heap of history">Ash heap of history</a></div>
<p>Since the 20th century, Western historians have disavowed the aspiration to provide the "judgement of history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curran2000p413_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curran2000p413-85">[85]</a></sup> The goals of historical judgements or interpretations are separate to those of <a href="/wiki/Judgment_(law)" title="Judgment (law)">legal judgements</a>, that need to be formulated quickly after the events and be final.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curran2000p415_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curran2000p415-86">[86]</a></sup> A related issue to that of the judgement of history is that of <a href="/wiki/Collective_memory" title="Collective memory">collective memory</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pseudohistory">Pseudohistory</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">Pseudohistory</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">Pseudohistory</a> is a term applied to texts which purport to be historical in nature but which depart from standard <a href="/wiki/Historical_method" title="Historical method">historiographical conventions</a> in a way which undermines their conclusions. It is closely related to deceptive <a href="/wiki/Historical_negationism" title="Historical negationism">historical revisionism</a>. Works which draw controversial conclusions from new, speculative, or disputed historical evidence, particularly in the fields of national, political, military, and religious affairs, are often rejected as pseudohistory.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Teaching">Teaching</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarship_vs_teaching">Scholarship vs teaching</span></h3>
<p>A major intellectual battle took place in Britain in the early twentieth century regarding the place of history teaching in the universities. At Oxford and Cambridge, scholarship was downplayed. Professor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Harding_Firth" title="Charles Harding Firth">Charles Harding Firth</a>, Oxford's Regius Professor of history in 1904 ridiculed the system as best suited to produce superficial journalists. The Oxford tutors, who had more votes than the professors, fought back in defense of their system saying that it successfully produced Britain's outstanding statesmen, administrators, prelates, and diplomats, and that mission was as valuable as training scholars. The tutors dominated the debate until after the Second World War. It forced aspiring young scholars to teach at outlying schools, such as Manchester University, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Frederick_Tout" title="Thomas Frederick Tout">Thomas Frederick Tout</a> was professionalizing the History undergraduate programme by introducing the study of original sources and requiring the writing of a thesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, scholarship was concentrated at the major PhD-producing universities, while the large number of other colleges and universities focused on undergraduate teaching. A tendency in the 21st century was for the latter schools to increasingly demand scholarly productivity of their younger tenure-track faculty. Furthermore, universities have increasingly relied on inexpensive part-time adjuncts to do most of the classroom teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalism">Nationalism</span></h3>
<p>From the origins of national school systems in the 19th century, the teaching of history to promote national sentiment has been a high priority. In the United States after World War I, a strong movement emerged at the university level to teach courses in Western Civilization, so as to give students a common heritage with Europe. In the U.S. after 1980, attention increasingly moved toward teaching <a href="/wiki/World_history" title="World history">world history</a> or requiring students to take courses in non-western cultures, to prepare students for life in a globalized economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the university level, historians debate the question of whether history belongs more to social science or to the humanities. Many view the field from both perspectives.
</p><p>The teaching of history in French schools was influenced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Nouvelle_histoire" title="Nouvelle histoire">Nouvelle histoire</a></i> as disseminated after the 1960s by <i>Cahiers pédagogiques and Enseignement</i> and other journals for teachers. Also influential was the Institut national de recherche et de documentation pédagogique, (INRDP). Joseph Leif, the Inspector-general of teacher training, said pupils children should learn about historians' approaches as well as facts and dates. Louis François, Dean of the History/Geography group in the Inspectorate of National Education advised that teachers should provide historic documents and promote "active methods" which would give pupils "the immense happiness of discovery." Proponents said it was a reaction against the memorization of names and dates that characterized teaching and left the students bored. Traditionalists protested loudly it was a postmodern innovation that threatened to leave the youth ignorant of French patriotism and national identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bias_in_school_teaching">Bias in school teaching</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Historybooks.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historybooks.JPG/220px-Historybooks.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historybooks.JPG/330px-Historybooks.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historybooks.JPG/440px-Historybooks.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Historybooks.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>History books in a bookstore</div></div></div>
<p>In several countries history textbooks are tools to foster nationalism and patriotism, and give students the official narrative about national enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many countries, history textbooks are sponsored by the national government and are written to put the national heritage in the most favorable light. For example, in Japan, mention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanking_Massacre" title="Nanking Massacre">Nanking Massacre</a> has been removed from textbooks and the entire Second World War is given cursory treatment. Other countries have complained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Another example includes Turkey, where there is no mention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide" title="Armenian Genocide">Armenian Genocide</a> in Turkish textbooks as a result of the denial of the genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was standard policy in communist countries to present only a rigid Marxist historiography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, textbooks published by the same company often differ in content from state to state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> An example of content that is represented different in different regions of the country is the history of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern states</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> are treated as controversial topics. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McGraw-Hill_Education" title="McGraw-Hill Education">McGraw-Hill Education</a> for example, was criticized for describing Africans brought to American plantations as "workers" instead of slaves in a textbook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Academic historians have often fought against the politicization of the textbooks, sometimes with success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 21st-century Germany, the history curriculum is controlled by the 16 states, and is characterized not by superpatriotism but rather by an "almost pacifistic and deliberately unpatriotic undertone" and reflects "principles formulated by international organizations such as UNESCO or the Council of Europe, thus oriented towards human rights, democracy and peace." The result is that "German textbooks usually downplay national pride and ambitions and aim to develop an understanding of citizenship centered on democracy, progress, human rights, peace, tolerance and Europeanness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Marx makes no claim to have produced a master key to history. Historical materialism is not "an historico-philosophic theory of the marche generale imposed by fate upon every people, whatever the historic circumstances in which it finds itself" (Marx, Karl: Letter to editor of the Russian paper <i>Otetchestvennye Zapiskym</i>, 1877). His ideas, he explains, are based on a concrete study of the actual conditions that pertained in Europe.</span>
</li>
